Those with two A-Levels will also qualify, but the scheme will exclude those who only have GCSEs. He wants to end the "barely concealed snobbery" about apprenticeships, which he claims comes from adults, rather than young people. The Lib Dems have gone to great lengths to point out the two million apprenticeships available under the coalition, stating that he and Vince Cable have been instrumental in pushing for them. The party told Newsbeat it wants to double that number in the next parliament. UKIP wants to introduce an option for students to take an apprenticeship (with a qualification at the end) instead of four non-core GCSE subjects. The employers they work with will be qualified to test and grade the apprentices, and the students can carry on working with them through their A-levels. The party also promises to scrap the target of 50% of school leavers going on to university, while scrapping tuition fees for some students taking degrees in high-demand vocational subjects like maths, technology, engineering, medicine and science - so long as they live, work and pay taxes in the UK for five years after they graduate. The Green Party hasn't published a specific policy on apprenticeships, but told Newsbeat: "[We] would expand apprenticeships by increasing funding. "We would ensure the government provides an apprenticeship to all qualified young people aged 16-25 who do not have one and want one. "{We would implement] a qualifications framework that would allow a real choice of academic and vocational areas, or a mixture of both. "With students encouraged to have an understanding of a wide range of subjects, and allowed to pursue areas where their talents lie, they will have the best possible start to their careers." The SNP aims to provide 125,000 "modern" apprenticeships in the next parliament and wants as many of the new jobs as possible to be in emerging high-tech industries. Plaid Cymru wants to promote higher level apprenticeships and in-work learning for young people and adults. Sir John (now Lord) Stevens, a former Metropolitan Police Commissioner, defined collusion as ranging from the wilful failure to keep records, the absence of accountability, the withholding of intelligence and evidence, to the extreme of agents being involved in murder. Canadian judge Peter Cory defined collusion as having a secret understanding, deliberately ignoring, overlooking, disregarding, passing over, taking no notice of, turning a blind eye, winking, excusing, condoning, looking the other way, and letting something ride. Nuala (now Baroness) O'Loan, the first police ombudsman for Northern Ireland, adopted the definitions of Lord Stevens and Peter Cory. Al Hutchinson, her successor, applied a less broad definition in his report. Sir Christopher de Silva provided a working definition of collusion that is less broad than that of Peter Cory and more in line with the Billy Wright inquiry panel. In Output (a), the summary accurately represents the article's discussion of "broken heart syndrome" or stress cardiomyopathy, which is a temporary condition where the heart muscle becomes weakened or stunned due to significant emotional or physical stress, such as bereavement. Dr. Sunil Shah of St George's at the University of London, one of the authors of a study published in the journal JAMA Internal Medicine, explains that the study found that the number of people who had a heart attack or stroke in the month after a loved one died was double that of a non-grieving control group. The British Heart Foundation also mentions this condition, stating that it is brought on by a shock, such as bereavement, and can cause the left ventricle of the heart to balloon out into the shape of a round-bottomed, narrow-necked vessel, known as a takotsubo cardiomyopathy. The article also highlights instances of couples passing away together, suggesting that their love endured until the end.
